Modeling of Slotted Cold-Formed Steel Members
Preface
Preface
This project was written as a Master Thesis in order to obtain a Master degree in Civil Engineering
at the Technical University of Denmark. The work was done under supervision of Professor Jeppe
J√∂nsson and Associate Professor Michael J. Andreassen.
The work was performed in the spring 2016 at DTU with a project weighting of 30 ECTS points.
Expression of my gratitude to Knauf A/S for their cooperation and to Jens Ole Frederiksen for his
helpfulness. A great job was done by S√łren Storm in reviewing the thesis.
Prof. Schafer has assisted me with calculations, and computational power was granted by prof. Guest
and Prof. Schafer, and not to forget, prof. Jeppe J√∂nsson for excellent counseling throughout this
project.
Astrid Winther Fischer
Kgs. Lyngby, June 2016

Modeling of Slotted Cold-Formed Steel Members
Abstract
Abstract
Cold-formed steel proÔ¨Āles have been used for construction for more than a century, but the Ô¨Ārst
standard was not published until 1940, where funding for research in cold-formed steel was granted by
AISI. This resulted in a constant supply of new standards for the use on cold-formed steel sections.
Here in Scandinavia the slotted cold-formed steel proÔ¨Āles are a more frequently used concept than the
‚Äôsolid‚Äô proÔ¨Āles, due to the enhanced thermal properties, which play an essential role in the building
norm here in the Northern Europe.
Multiple methods suitable for the slotted proÔ¨Āles have been
introduced in recent decades, but none have taken root in the European standard. Several studies
have shown that slotted proÔ¨Āles behave diÔ¨Äerently from solid proÔ¨Āles, subsequently, illumination of
these proÔ¨Āles is necessary and speciÔ¨Āc rules should be introduced in standards with calculation methods
for slotted cold-formed steel members.
This project will handle several aspects of slotted cold-formed proÔ¨Āles in an attempt to give a useful
set of calculation methods that Ô¨Āt the format of Eurocode 3. The models will eventually be used with
Eurocode 3, and compared with test results of various slotted C-proÔ¨Āles.
The rotation stiÔ¨Äness of the slotted cold-formed web has been developed by former students at DTU
BYG, but was in this project derived to a compact, useful expression. The formula has been veriÔ¨Āed by
comparison to FEM models of slotted webs with varying dimensions. In addition, another expression
for the rotation stiÔ¨Äness is derived for use in members subjected to bending. The term was likewise
compared and veriÔ¨Āed by use of FEM models. Both terms for the rotation stiÔ¨Äness are proving to be
good estimates.
FEM models have provided the foundation for the expression of a shear modulus of the slotted part
of the web, which then has been compared to several FEM models in order to verify the expression
for the shear modulus. It turns out that the term estimates a stiÔ¨Äness in the appropriate magnitude,
but does not provide the proper stiÔ¨Äness. Subsequently, small changes in the expression were made
but did not result in improvements.
A study of the stability for an orthotropic plate was carried out, where both the critical stress for
compression in one direction and the critical shear stress were derived. An expression of the bending
stiÔ¨Äness in the longitudinal and transverse direction was derived and utilized for comparison of esti-
mated critical stresses with FEM results of a slotted body in one axis compression and shear loading.
Surprisingly, it turned out that the ‚Äôsolid‚Äô isotropic plate gives the best result for one axis compression,
while the orthotropic plate (slotted web) yields the best estimate of the critical shear stress.
Several compression tests of slotted proÔ¨Āles were carried out at DTU Civil Engineering. The experi-
mental results were compared to estimated capacities of the very same sections with diÔ¨Äerent variations
of Eurocode 3. It turns out that the use of Eurocode 3 including the slotted web‚Äôs rotational stiÔ¨Äness
generally gives slightly conservative capacities of the proÔ¨Āles. Whereas, using Eurocode 3 without
changes results in slightly too large capacities when compared to experimental test results. Including
the shear stiÔ¨Äness for determining a reduction factor for the buckling load reduces the capacity and
provides slightly better estimates, but not a signiÔ¨Ācant improvement.
Experimental tests on entire wall sections with attached plasterboards were executed and compared
to the estimated bearing capacities of the entire wall section. The results are comparable, but due to
the gypsum boards, the estimated values are merely estimates of the actual resistance of the tested
specimens.

Abstract
Resum√©
Kold-formede st√•lproÔ¨Āler har v√¶ret brugt i konstruktioner i mere end et √•rhundrede, men den f√łrste
standard blev f√łrst oÔ¨Äentliggjort i 1940, hvor AISI stod bag en intens st√łtte til forskning i kold-formede
st√•lproÔ¨Āler. Dette resulterede i en j√¶vn tilf√łrsel af nye standarder til brug for kold-formede st√•lproÔ¨Āler.
Her i Skandinavien bruges slidsede kold-formede proÔ¨Āler i st√łrre stil end hele proÔ¨Āler, p√• grund af de
forbedrede termiske egenskaber, som spiller en v√¶sentlig rolle i byggenormen her i Norden. Flere
beregningsmetoder til brug for de slidsede proÔ¨Āler er blevet introduceret i de seneste √•rtier, men ingen
har fundet rodf√¶ste i den Europ√¶iske standard. Flere unders√łgelser har dog vist at slidsede proÔ¨Āler
opf√łrer sig forskelligt fra hele proÔ¨Āler, hvorfor belysning af disse proÔ¨Āler er n√łdvendig ligesom s√¶rlige
regler b√łr indf√łres i standarder med beregningsmetoder af slidsede kold-formede proÔ¨Āler.
Dette projekt vil h√•ndtere Ô¨āere forskellige aspekter af slidsede kold-formede proÔ¨Āler i et fors√łg p√• at
komme med et brugbart s√¶t beregningsmodeller, der passer til Eurocode 3 formatet. Modellerne vil i
sidste ende blive brugt med Eurocode 3, og sammenlignet med testfors√łg p√• diverse slidsede C-proÔ¨Āler.
En drejningsstivhed for den slidsede kold-formede krop er blevet udviklet af tidligere DTU BYG
studerende, men er her blevet udledt til et kompakt, brugbart udtryk, som er blevet holdt op imod
FEM modeller af slidsede kroppe med varierende dimensioner af slidserne. Derudover er et andet
udtryk for drejningsstivheden udledt til brug for proÔ¨Āler der er b√łjningsbelastede.
Udtrykket er
ligeledes blevet sammenlignet med tilsvarende FEM modeller. Begge udtryk for drejningsstivheden
viser sig at v√¶re gode estimater.
FEM modeller har lagt bunden for et udtryk for et forskydningsmodul af den slidsede del af proÔ¨Ālen,
som derefter er blevet sammenlignet med Ô¨āere FEM modeller for at veriÔ¨Ācere udtrykket for forskyd-
ningsmodulet. Det viser sig, at udtrykket estimerer en stivhed i den rette st√łrrelsesorden, men ikke
giver den rigtige stivhed for den slidsede krop. Efterf√łlgende sm√• √¶ndringer af udtrykket har ikke ledt
til forbedringer.
En unders√łgelse af pladestabiliteten for en ortotrop plade blev udf√łrt, hvor b√•de den kritiske sp√¶nding
for tryk i en retning og den kritiske forskydningssp√¶nding blev udledt. Et udtryk for b√łjningsstivhe-
den i den langs- og tv√¶rg√•ende retning er pr√¶senteret og brugt i forbindelse med sammenligning af
estimerede kritiske sp√¶ndinger med FEM resultater af en slidset krop i √©n-akset tryk og i forskydning.
Overraskende viser det sig at v√¶re en hel isotrop plade der giver det bedste result for √©n-akset tryk,
mens den ortotrope plade (slidset krop) giver det bedste estimat for den kritiske forskydningssp√¶nding.
Flere forskellige trykfors√łg af slidsede proÔ¨Āler er blevet udf√łrt ved DTU BYG. Fors√łgsresultaterne er
blevet sammenlignet med ansl√•ede b√¶reevner af de selvsamme proÔ¨Āler med forskellige varianter af Eu-
rocode 3. Det viser sig, at brug af Eurocode 3 med brug af den slidsede krops drejningsstivhed generelt
giver konservative b√¶reevner af proÔ¨Ālerne. Hvorimod brug af Eurocode 3 uden √¶ndringer resulterer
i lidt for store b√¶reevner, i forhold til testede resultater. Ved inddragelse af forskydningsstivheden
til bestemmelse af en reduktionsfaktor for bulelasten, mindskes b√¶reevnen og giver en anelse bedre
estimater, men ikke en m√¶rkbar forbedring.
Fors√łg p√• hele v√¶gsektioner med fastgjorte gipsplader har v√¶ret udf√łrt og er blevet sammenlignet
med estimerede b√¶reevner af hele v√¶gsektionen. Resultaterne er til at sammenligne, men p√• grund
af gipspladerne, er de beregnede v√¶rdier mere et estimat end et egentligt udtryk for b√¶reevnen.

==PAGE:13==
Modeling of Slotted Cold-Formed Steel Members
Introduction
1
Introduction
Cold-formed steel (CFS) is a construction concept, comprised of steel sheets folded into steel members
that can easily be assembled at the construction site or be pre-assembled to whole wall or Ô¨āoor sections.
CFS is an alternative to timber constructions, both having similar structural properties, giving rise to
the same structural build-up. CFS members are relatively light and can to some extent be managed
by one person. Figure 1.1 is an illustration of a CFS multi-story construction.
Figure 1.1: Multi-story cold-formed building in Federal Hill, Baltimore US.
Cold-formed steel members are manufactured from steel sheets, which have been formed either by
rollering or by pressing, leading to the desired geometry of a CFS member.
The folding process
only permits open cross-sections, but is not limited to a speciÔ¨Āc geometry, see Figure 1.2. Closed
cross-sections can be achieved by assembling two or more single members into one built-up member.
Figure 1.2: Selection of most frequently used and possible CFS cross-sections. From DS/EN 1993-1-3 (2007).
1
==PAGE:14==
Modeling of Slotted Cold-Formed Steel Members
Introduction
Cold-formed steel structures have been used in the past several decades in America, since their in-
troduction in the AISI SpeciÔ¨Ācation in 1946. However, the use of CFS was employed even before
this date, primarily in the car industry as bodywork and in the home applications industry (washing
machines, etc). But the years following the Great Depression made the market for the cars and home
applications suÔ¨Äer, due to a lack of consumers of these products. In contrast, construction work did
not lack support during this period, due to governmental investments in building. Consequently, CFS
found its way into the construction industry. However, the absence of building codes for CFS meant
that they could not be of use immediately, why AISI invested in research at Cornell University in 1939
(AISI (2016) and Winter, White, and Gallagher (1972)).
Cold-formed steel members have many advantages: High strength, eÔ¨Écient production, recyclability,
minimum on-site labor requirements and a lightness of elements. However, the latter is equivalent
to stability problems associated with thin-walled beams. All materials suÔ¨Äer from stability in the
shape of lateral or Euler buckling, and some building elements even have local buckling issues. But
in addition, thin-walled members such as CFS also suÔ¨Äer from distortional buckling. Simple web and
edge stiÔ¨Äeners make the CFS members more resistant to local and distortional buckling.
Figure 1.3: Failure modes for slotted cold-formed steel members according to H√∂glund (1998a). Buckling pictures
from, Fernandes (-), ESDEP (2016), and Martins et al. (2015).
In Scandinavia the slotted CFS member is a more favorable product, due to its better thermal prop-
erties. With improved thermal properties the CFS member is a competitive alternative to timber
structures in all matters. A comparison between two identical houses has been made by Ife (1975),
where the only diÔ¨Äerence between the houses was the structural material: wooden studs or slotted
CFS studs. The observations of the two houses occurred during the construction period and through
three years in which the houses were inhabited. The Ô¨Ānal remark on the study was that the steel
products performed in a very satisfactory manner.
The perforations in the slotted CFS member are typically longitudinal holes that are cut in the steel
sheets before the roll-forming process. The slotted CFS member faces some of the same problems as
a solid CFS member, but because of the reduced strength of the web, the slotted member will behave
2
==PAGE:15==
Modeling of Slotted Cold-Formed Steel Members
Introduction
diÔ¨Äerently and have other failure modes.
H√∂glund (1998a) mentions seven failure modes for slotted CFS members subjected to axial and trans-
verse loading, the failures are illustrated in Figure 1.3.
1. Euler buckling in the plane of the web
2. Lateral buckling of the compression Ô¨āange
3. Shear failure in the slotted web
4. Failure due to support reactions
5. Failure under concentrated forces
6. Buckling of the compression Ô¨āange‚Äôs edge
7. Buckling of the compression Ô¨āange‚Äôs edge at the support
The failure modes 4, 5, and 7 are collapses due to concentrated forces and failure mode 6 is a local
web failure causing the compression Ô¨āange to buckle. In this project failure mode 1, 2, and 3 will
be discussed, which are failures due to global stability and distortional buckling of parts that are in
compression, and shear failure of the web.
Slotted CFS members are likewise not limited to a certain section‚Äôs conÔ¨Āgurations, however the part
with perforations is typically placed on a Ô¨āat part of the member. Likewise, the perforations of the
members can take various shapes, distribution and spread: some members have one row of holes in
the transverse direction (Figure 1.4), for lateral bracing of the columns, others have several rows of
holes, like in Figure 1.5. The slots in the Ô¨Āgure are rectangular, but perforations can also be circular
or any other shape, which can be cut or punched in the steel sheets. In this project, perforations,
as illustrated in Figure 1.5, will be considered, which is favorable for reducing the thermal bridge in
external walls. The sizes of the slots can vary, but typically they will have a length of about 70mm,
with a longitudinal distance of 25mm, a transverse distance at 6-9mm, and a slot width of 3mm
(H√∂glund (1997)).
Figure 1.4: Example of alternative conÔ¨Āguration
of slots, from https: // www. pinterest. com
Figure 1.5: Slotted cold-formed steel track member,
from Jacobsen (2015b).
3
==PAGE:16==
Modeling of Slotted Cold-Formed Steel Members
Introduction
1.1
Research
Today, the design methods for ‚Äôsolid‚Äô CFS members are the EÔ¨Äective Width Method (EWM) and Direct
Strength Method (DSM). The former method is practiced in Europe while both methods are included
in the building codes in America and Australia (AS/NZS 4600:2005 (2005)).
The EÔ¨Äective Width method was Ô¨Ārst introduced by Von Karman and was later modiÔ¨Āed by Winter
to the format that is used today. The method considers a plate‚Äôs reduced stress distribution during
buckling, but instead of having diÔ¨Äerent stress values in the cross-section an eÔ¨Äective width/area is
used with a ‚Äôconstant‚Äô stress throughout the cross-section. ESDEP (2016)
The Direct Strength method was developed for replacement of the EÔ¨Äective Width method, for es-
caping the cumbersome calculations of eÔ¨Äective widths. The method was introduced by Schafer and
Pek√∂z (1998b) and has been modiÔ¨Āed to the format that is used today. This method includes inter-
actions between buckling modes and is an empirical design formula. The literature study of slotted
cold-formed steel members (see Appendix H) found that this method was the most reliable method
for predicting the strength of solid CFS members.
Currently, there is no design code for the slotted cold-formed steel members, however, several sugges-
tions for predicting the strength of perforated members have been introduced. Kesti suggested to use
an eÔ¨Äective thickness of the slotted part of the web for use in Eurocode, while H√∂glund introduced a
new set of design procedures for slotted members, handling several diÔ¨Äerent failure modes (H√∂glund
(1997) and Kesti and Makelainen (1999b)).
1.2
Problem
The goal for this project is to set up models and expressions for determining the bearing capacity of
slotted cold-formed steel members, in axial compression, major/minor bending and for shear loading.
In this project, an analytical expression for the distortional buckling of slotted CFS members will be
set up and compared to FEM models. The analytical expression will to the possible extent Ô¨Āt into the
current guidelines in Eurocode 3 and will be able to handle members subjected to axial compression
and bending.
Beam-columns will be subjected to transverse loading, resulting in essential estimation of the shear
capacity, which is observed to be remarkably reduced for slotted CFS members compared to solid
CFS members. Analytical formulas for the shear capacity of slotted CFS members will be set up
and compared to FEM models. Shear loaded slotted CFS members have previously been tested, and
failure modes and ultimate loads can be compared to the proposed models. Proposals for Ô¨Ātting the
analytical expression to the FEM models will be made, if the FEM model is detailed enough and
provides desirable results.
Finally, a combination of axial compression, bending and shear loading of a slotted CFS member will
be investigated, with the Ô¨Ānal goal in mind: providing a suitable strength model for slotted CFS
members.
4
==PAGE:17==
Modeling of Slotted Cold-Formed Steel Members
Introduction
1.3
Structure of Thesis
The perforations aÔ¨Äect the web‚Äôs bending stiÔ¨Äness, which will have an impact on the distortional
buckling load. This will be discussed in Section 3 were a model for the spring stiÔ¨Äness of the perforated
web will be presented and validated.
The perforations also aÔ¨Äect the shear capacity of the web, why a reduced shear modulus will be
introduced in Section 4.
A Ô¨Ānal investigation will be conducted on the slotted web‚Äôs stability issues, where the perforated web
will be considered as an orthotropic material, with diÔ¨Äerent bending stiÔ¨Äness in each direction and a
reduced shear stiÔ¨Äness. The stability problems will be discussed in Section 5.
It is desired that these new aspects of stiÔ¨Äness of slotted members will provide Eurocode 3 with a
new set of guidelines for calculations of the bearing capacity of slotted CFS members. Section 6 will
present a comparison between experimental test results and the proposed models, the performance of
the models in connection with design purposes will be investigated and discussed. Primary compression
tests have been executed, but also a few tests of members subjected to transverse load are available.
A Ô¨Ānal summarizing of the recommended design models for slotted cold-formed steel members will be
listed in Section 7.
5
==PAGE:18==
Modeling of Slotted Cold-Formed Steel Members
Background
2
Background
2.1
Cold-Formed Steel Members
Cold-formed steel members have been proven to buckle, and the buckling modes can be divided into
three categories: Local, distortional and global buckling.
Local buckling is characterized by the relative short wavelengths and buckling of individual plate
elements. The junctions between elements keep their location during local buckling, so the section
appears to retain its shape, see Figure 2.1a and 2.2a.
Local buckling will not cause the member to collapse, but it does aÔ¨Äect the ultimate capacity of
the member. Loading beyond the local buckling state is called post-buckling, and the post-buckled
capacity of a member can be estimated with the eÔ¨Äective width method.
Distortional buckling can be recognized when both translation and rotation of the compression fold
line between Ô¨āange and lip occur, see Figure 2.1b and 2.2b. Distortional buckling can cause large
deÔ¨āections, which lead to failure.
When the cross-section undergoes rigid body rotation or translation the buckling mode is categorized
as global buckling, see Figure 2.1c and 2.2c.
Figure 2.1: Buckling modes for a lipped channel member subjected to axial compression, a) local, b) distortional,
and c) global buckling. From Fischer and Schafer (2016)
Figure 2.2: Buckling modes for a lipped channel member subjected to major bending, a) local, b) distortional,
and c) global buckling. From Fischer and Schafer (2016)
6
==PAGE:19==
Modeling of Slotted Cold-Formed Steel Members
Background
2.2
The Slotted Members
The steel proÔ¨Āle investigated in this project is the lipped channel member with perforations in the
web part. The layout of the C-section and the perforations is shown in Figure 2.3. The perforations
in this project will overlap each other, meaning that cs > 2 fs.
All dimensions associated with the perforations are illustrated in Figure 2.3b, and the dimensions in a
lipped channel member are illustrated in Figure 2.3a. Note that the notation illustrated in the Ô¨Āgures
will be used in the calculations in this project.
Figure 2.3: a) Cross-section of lip channel member with perforation in the web, and b) Layout of the perforations
in the web. Notations are used throughout this project. From H√∂glund (1998a).
2.3
Design Codes
As previously mentioned, two methods dominate the design codes for cold-formed steel sections in
Europe, North America and Australia: The eÔ¨Äective width method and the direct strength method.
Both methods estimate the bearing capacity with some deviation from the actual capacity (see Section
2.3.3).
However, in this project the eÔ¨Äective width method, as described in Eurocode 3, will be subjected to
modiÔ¨Ācations for including slotted CFS members in the design code.
2.3.1
Eurocode Design Procedure
The design code for steel structures in Europe is described in Eurocode 3, where Part 1-1 is general
rules for steel structures, Part 1-3 is for cold-formed steel members, and Part 1-5 is for plated structural
elements. The part for plates describes the eÔ¨Äective width method and the design procedures for steel
members, which can be split into plate elements, while Part 1-3 includes additional design procedures
for cold-formed steel.
Cold-formed steel members typically have distortional buckling issues, due to the layout of the member.
Therefore, the Eurocode concerning cold-formed steel members has incorporated a reduction factor
speciÔ¨Ācally for the distortional buckling mode.
7
==PAGE:20==
Modeling of Slotted Cold-Formed Steel Members
Background
The eÔ¨Äective width method reÔ¨āects the bearing parts of the member undergoing local buckling. Local
buckling causes a stiÔ¨Äness reduction at the middle of the plate elements, but almost no eÔ¨Äects are seen
at the junctions between elements.
A reduction factor (Ōá) for the distortional mode is determined through an iteration process, and is
only a reduction of the parts that are most aÔ¨Äected by distortional buckling, meaning the parts that
undergo large deÔ¨āections, see Figure 2.1b and 2.2b. For a lipped channel member, these are the outer
eÔ¨Äective part of the Ô¨āange and the eÔ¨Äective part of the lip, see the black elements inside the circle in
Figure 2.4.
Figure 2.4: EÔ¨Äective part of Ô¨āange and lip, nota-

The initial step for estimating the ultimate capacity of a CFS member according to Eurocode 3 is
to calculate the gross sectional properties of the whole member, followed by determining the eÔ¨Äective
widths for each element (web, Ô¨āanges and lips). To obtain the reduction factor of the plate widths,
the critical buckling stress (Equation 2.1) is needed, which comprises an elastic plate buckling stress,
which can be found in Eurocode 3 Part 1-5 Appendix 1, and a buckling factor. An estimate for the
critical buckling stress for the slotted web, considering the reduced stiÔ¨Äness in the web plate due to
the slots, will be discussed later in this project.

paragraph 6.3.2.2(1) in EC3-1-1, using an imperfection factor őĪLT = 0.34. Mcr is determined according
to chapter 4.3 in Gimsing (1980). However, lateral-tortional buckling is not concern for CFS members,
because it is assumed that the member is restrained against lateral torsional buckling due to the general
construction metgod for CFS members: The member will be screwed to sheathings with regularly
intervals on one side or both sides of the member. Therefore, is ŌáLT = 1 for the calculations.

2.3.2
Direct Strength Method
The direct strength method (DSM) is an empirical set of formulas for determining the Ô¨Ānal strength
capacity of a CFS member. The method considers interactions between the buckling modes, resulting
in a more precise prediction of the member‚Äôs strength.
The initial step is to determine all three critical buckling loads (local, distortional and global) for the
member, which can be done in various ways, but AISI suggests to use Cufsm (Li and Schafer (2010)).
Cufsm is a software package which runs a Ô¨Ānite strip analysis of a given cross-section. The program
returns the signature curve for the member, illustrated in Figure 2.7, where the critical buckling loads
for the local and distortional modes can be extracted at the minima, while for global buckling the
critical load is at the location corresponding to the length of the member.
Figure 2.7:
Signature curve for a
lipped channel member in major axis
bending, where the local and distor-
tional buckling minima are clearly de-
Ô¨Āned.
Figure 2.8: Signature curve for a lipped channel member subjected to
axial compression and cFSM curves for local, distortional and global
buckling.
Figure 2.7 illustrates the signature curve for a lipped channel member in pure bending and has a
distinct minimum for local and distortional buckling. Unfortunately, not all curves have such distinct
minima, which is illustrated in the signature curve in Figure 2.8, where the distortional minimum is
not clearly deÔ¨Āned. In such cases, constrained Ô¨Ānite strip analysis (cFSM) is used to Ô¨Ānd the location
of the minimum, followed by ordinary FS analysis to estimate the size of the buckling loads.
In
Figure 2.8, the constrained Ô¨Ānite strip curves for local, distortional and global buckling are illustrated
together with the ordinary Ô¨Ānite strip curve.
1The notation Mz,Rd is the resistance moment in the z-direction, also referred to as bending about the y-axis. This
notation is used throughout this project

2.4
Abaqus Models
All the proposed models are compared with Ô¨Ānite element results from Abaqus. The Abaqus models
are generated through Python scripts, which include a for loop over the input variables, meaning
that if 10 diÔ¨Äerent layouts of the slotted web are represented in the inputs, the script will run the
routine 10 times.
It is possible to submit Python scripts to the no-gui interface of Abaqus on a computer cluster
(super-computer), this is beneÔ¨Ācial because the super-computer is in general faster than an ordinary
computer, and after submitting the script it does not require attention and will be running till it is
done on the external computer without any interferes.
The Python scripts are included in Appendix F.
2.4.1
General Abaqus Inputs
This project contains many Abaqus models, and several inputs are consistent through all the models.
‚ÄĘ All inputs to Abaqus is in SI-units, (m, N, Pa, etc.)
‚ÄĘ Young‚Äôs modulus: E = 210GPa
‚ÄĘ Poisson‚Äôs ratio: őĹ = 0.3
‚ÄĘ The material is homogeneous
‚ÄĘ Models are modeled as a 3D Planar Shell with thickness t = 1mm
‚ÄĘ The thickness integration rule is Simpson with 5 integration points
‚ÄĘ The step is either
‚Äď General Static Step, with initial increment=0.1 and maximum increment=1
‚Äď Buckle Step, where the number of eigenvalues and vectors depend on the problem, but num-
ber of values are between 3 and 10. Maximum number of iterations are likewise depending
on the problem, but ranges between 100-100000
‚ÄĘ Both steps do not consider nonlinear eÔ¨Äects due to large displacements (nlgeom)
‚ÄĘ The element type for the mesh is either
‚Äď Quadratic elements, with linear shape functions (S4R), and reduced integration
‚Äď Triangular elements, with quadratic shape functions (STRI65)
‚ÄĘ The mesh size depend on the problem
‚ÄĘ Boundary conditions are placed in the Initial step

stiÔ¨Äness, reduction in shear capacity and buckling stress of a slotted web. Four diÔ¨Äerent dimensions
were varied separately, cs, ds, es and so/su, in these studies and when a dimension was not varied,

The Slotted Web‚Äôs Bending StiÔ¨Äness
The stiÔ¨Äness of the web inÔ¨āuences the distortional buckling and is therefore part of the strength
capacity formula formulated in Eurocode 3 (Part 1-3) for cold-formed steel members. The spring
stiÔ¨Äness for a solid web can be derived from a simple supported beam subjected to constant moment,
see Figure 3.1, where the inverse rotation equals the rotational stiÔ¨Äness of the beam (applies for a
member in pure compression):
őł = 1

However, the stiÔ¨Äness of a perforated web is reduced due to the slots, and therefore a model for
the spring stiÔ¨Äness of a perforated web has been introduced by Shaker (2015a), Madsen (2015), and
Jacobsen (2015a).
Figure 3.1: Static model of the web subjected to a constant moment. This model can be applied for
members in pure compression.
3.1
Constant Moment in the Web - Member in Compression
The distortional buckling mode of a cold-formed steel member in pure compression is illustrated in
Figure 2.1b. This deformation mode can be obtained by applying a constant moment to the section‚Äôs
web, as illustrated in Figure 3.1, which leads to the spring stiÔ¨Äness in Equation 3.1, for a solid web.
The stiÔ¨Äness model of a slotted web by Shaker (2015a), Madsen (2015), and Jacobsen (2015a) is based
on this buckling mode and the constant moment in the web.
Figure 3.2: Normal stresses about the x-axis in a slot-
ted web subjected to constant moment.
Figure 3.3: Shear stresses in a slotted web subjected
to constant moment.
17
==PAGE:30==
Modeling of Slotted Cold-Formed Steel Members
The Slotted Web‚Äôs Bending StiÔ¨Äness
Figure 3.4: Model for the analytically formulation of the web‚Äôs rotation. From Shaker (2015a)
Figure 3.5: Model for the analytically formulation of the web‚Äôs rotation. Red, orange and blue strips
are subjected to bending in the y-axis, while the green strips are subjected to a twisting moment.
The model is based on observations of a Ô¨Ānite element structure of the slotted web subjected to
constant moment. The stress Ô¨Āeld outputs from Abaqus deÔ¨Āne the parts of the web that are subjected
to normal stresses (along the x-axis) and shear stresses, see Figure 3.2 and 3.3, respectively. From the
Ô¨Āgures, it is found that the shear stresses are present in the parts between the slots in the transverse
direction, while the normal stresses are mainly concentrated in an area also between the slots but in
the longitudinal direction. Because the stress components are separated from each other, a simple
model, as illustrated in Figure 3.4, can describe the deformations and rotation of the slotted web quite
precisely.
The model consists of four diÔ¨Äerent strips of diÔ¨Äerent widths and lengths. The deformations of each
strip can be determined with the principle of virtual work, Krenk and H√łgsberg (2013). Consequently,
a summation of the strips‚Äô deformations results in an estimate for the web‚Äôs total deformation.
The diÔ¨Äerent colors in Figure 3.5 specify the four diÔ¨Äerent strips: The red, orange and blue strips
are subjected to bending in the y-axis, while the green strips are subjected to pure torsion about the
x-axis.
The principle of the virtual work is to apply a virtual load on the structure, in this case a moment at
M1 = 1 at both ends of the web, where the rotations are desired (normally, only one virtual load is
applied, but due to symmetry the rotations at each end will be equal and will ease the calculations).
Internal as well as external work is performed on the web by the virtual motion, and the actual
18

loading of the web is included in the moment, M0. The total work on the proÔ¨Āle equals zero, followed
by determination of the web‚Äôs rotation:

Abaqus Model
A study of the proposed models‚Äô accuracy was executed through Abaqus, where the slotted web‚Äôs
dimensions are varied separately, and the number of slots along the height of the web is likewise varied.
The study includes four diÔ¨Äerent ratios between the dimensions: so‚ą®su

Two very similar Python scripts are made for getting a representation of the bending stiÔ¨Äness of the
perforated web. The only change to the two scripts is the loading along the bottom edge of the web:
In the case of a CFS member in compression, the web is subjected to moment along both edges, while
with the CFS member in major bending, the web is only subjected to moment along the web‚Äôs top
edge.

Shear Reduction
A plate undergoing pure shear deformation will have shear forces along the edges, as illustrated in
Figure 4.1. If a solid plate undergoes a vertical shear deformation of őīy = L, it will result in shear
stresses in the plate of the size:
ő≥ = őīy

Two Abaqus models are created, one of a solid plate and one of a slotted plate, both have the same
outer dimensions: L = 800mm, h = 180m and t = 1mm. Both plates are subjected to a vertical shear
deformation (őīy = L), resulting in the shear stresses in the solid plate listed above.
The forces acting along the edges of the solid plate will be:
Qx = ŌĄ t L = 64.62 MN
(4.2a)
Qy = ŌĄ t h = 14.54 MN
(4.2b)
The Abaqus model of the solid web yields the same reaction forces, however, the model of the slotted
web returns vertical reaction forces that are noticeably lower: Qy = 4.728 MN, which is about one
third of the vertical force from the solid web.
This is evidence that the web with perforations has a reduced shear stiÔ¨Äness and therefore also shear
capacity. In the following, an analytical expression for the shear modulus will be derived and the
performance of the expression will be compared to FEM models.

4.5

columns, which provides good estimates for many beams, because bending causes the major part of
the deÔ¨āection. But for beams with a small shear stiÔ¨Äness, this beam theory is no longer satisfactory,
and a shear term is needed in the diÔ¨Äerential equations.

listed below, and for the Bernoulli beam it results in the 4th order diÔ¨Äerential equation for an ideal

4.6
Abaqus Models for Verifying the Reduced Shear StiÔ¨Äness
Two studies of the slotted web are performed with Abaqus: One study of a simple supported slotted

web with axial compression and boundary conditions forcing the web to buckle in-plane.
Note that these Abaqus models have a solid part at each end of the web, see Figure 4.13 and 4.14.
This was done to secure proper load transfer and to minimize large deÔ¨āections due to small stiÔ¨Äness
of a strip and the large reaction forces. The solid part is 50mm long at each end.
The Abaqus models with simple supports and a uniform load applied have the following properties:
‚ÄĘ The web consists of twenty modules of slots in the longitudinal direction
‚ÄĘ The mesh of these models consists of the triangular element
‚ÄĘ The mesh size is deÔ¨Āned by a seed size of cs/20
‚ÄĘ The models are supported vertically (y-direction) at both ends along the whole height. A single
point at the left edge at mid height of the web is supported against horizontal (x-direction)
displacements.
‚ÄĘ A surface load is applied on the whole surface of the shell with magnitude p = 10[ N
m2 ] in the
y-direction.
The Euler buckling Abaqus models have the following properties:
‚ÄĘ The web consists of twenty modules of slots in the longitudinal direction
‚ÄĘ The mesh of these models consists of the triangular element
‚ÄĘ The mesh size is deÔ¨Āned by a seed size of es/4
‚ÄĘ A buckling step is used for this analysis
‚ÄĘ The models are supported vertically (y-direction) at both ends along the whole height. A single
point at the left edge at mid height of the web is supported against horizontal (x-direction)
displacements.
‚ÄĘ The whole surface of the web is restrained against displacements in the z-direction
‚ÄĘ A shell edge load is applied normal on the right end of the web with magnitude N = 1[ N
m2 ].
The Python scripts can be found in Appendix F.3 and F.4, for the simple supported beam and the

Modeling of Slotted Cold-Formed Steel Members
Plate Stability of the Slotted Web
5
Plate Stability of the Slotted Web
Local buckling does not necessarily mean failure, however it does aÔ¨Äect the ultimate capacity of the
member. When designing thin steel members, the post-buckling capacity is estimated, because it is
thought that the member has already buckled locally, and this is handled with the eÔ¨Äective width
method.
The eÔ¨Äective widths are based on the critical buckling stress of a plate:
ŌÉE = ŌÄ2 Ep t2
12 b2
(5.1a)
ŌÉcr = kŌÉ ŌÉE
(5.1b)
This formula is based on the isotropic plate theory, which both Timoshenko and Woinowsky-Krieger
(1959), Timoshenko (1936), and Troitsky (1976) describe.
However, a perforated web is not an
isotropic material, and it is thought that the slotted web can be better represented by an orthotropic
material.
In the following the buckling stress for an isotropic and orthotropic material will be derived for a plate
subjected to compression in one direction and shear load.
5.1
Isotopic Bending stiÔ¨Äness
A general formulation for the strain energy can be obtained, by considering pure bending and twisting
of a plate:
dUbending = ‚ąí

‚ąāx‚ąāy dx dy
(5.2b)
where Mi are the moments in diÔ¨Äerent direction and w is the deÔ¨āection of the plate.
For an isotropic plate, the bending moments are:
Mx = ‚ąíD

5.5
Abaqus Model
Two studies of the derived buckling stresses‚Äô accuracy for an orthotropic plate are carried out with
comparison to Abaqus models, where the dimensions of the slotted web are varied separately together
with the number of slots along the height of the web. The dimensions of the study are described in
Section 2.4.2. The deformed state of the models is illustrated in Figure 5.3 and 5.4.
Two similar Python scripts are made for the buckling analysis of a plate in one axis compression and
in shear loading along all edges. Note again that the models have a solid part at each end of the web,
the width of one solid part is 50mm.
For the models in one axis compression the properties are:
‚ÄĘ The web consists of Ô¨Āfty modules of slots in the longitudinal direction, to insure that the length
does not inÔ¨āuence the buckling stress
‚ÄĘ The mesh of these models consists of the triangular element
‚ÄĘ The mesh size is deÔ¨Āned with a seed size of 0.01
‚ÄĘ The models are supported along all edges in the z-direction, simply supported at the left end,
and supported against displacements in the y-direction at the right end
‚ÄĘ The buckling step is used
‚ÄĘ The load is applied at the right end in the x-direction with magnitude N = 1[ N
m]
For the models loaded in shear the properties are:
‚ÄĘ The web consists of Ô¨Āve modules of slots in the longitudinal direction
‚ÄĘ The mesh of these models consists of the triangular element
‚ÄĘ The mesh size is deÔ¨Āned with a seed size of 0.005
‚ÄĘ The models are supported along all edges in the z-direction, simply supported at the bottom
left corner, and supported against displacements in the y-direction at the right bottom corner
‚ÄĘ The buckling step is used
‚ÄĘ The load is applied along all the edges, with direction corresponding to shear load and with
magnitude Nxy = 1[ N
m]
The scripts can be found in Appendix F.5 and F.6.

5.5.1
Comparison with Abaqus Model
For the slotted webs in one axis compression, the critical stress values for the isotropic material match
the results from Abaqus quite well, see Figure 5.5. While the results for the orthotropic plate yield
much lower critical stress estimates than the Abaqus results, see Figure 5.6. For both Ô¨Āgures, the
blue circles indicate results from Abaqus and the full lines are the calculated buckling stresses using
Equation 5.13 or 5.15.
However, the formula for the buckling stress in an isotropic plate does estimate the stress slightly
greater than the stress from the Abaqus models. This can be expected, because the slotted web has
a reduction in stiÔ¨Äness, compared to the solid web that was used for estimating the critical stresses.
Surprisingly, for the shear stability of a slotted web, the buckling stress formula for the orthotropic
plate gives the most accurate results when compared to the Abaqus models, see Figure 5.7. The
results using the isotropic buckling stress give estimates about Ô¨Āve times larger than the Abaqus
results.
Finally, the diÔ¨Äerence between the results from Abaqus and the buckling compression stress for an
isotropic plate and the buckling shear stress for an orthotropic plate is illustrated in Figure 5.8. Where
the full lines are the shear stresses, and the dotted lines indicate the ratio between the compression
stresses.

Employing ModiÔ¨Ācations into Eurocode 3
6
Employing ModiÔ¨Ācations into Eurocode 3
In this section the previously investigated aspects of slotted cold-formed steel will be incorporated
into the Eurocode 3 calculations and compared to test results performed by previous students at DTU
BYG and KTH Royal Institute of Technology, and by Teknologisk Institut.
The initial thought was to employ three modiÔ¨Ācations to the Eurocode: Rotational spring stiÔ¨Äness of
the web, reduced shear stiÔ¨Äness in the Euler buckling load, and Ô¨Ānally, the critical buckling stress of
the perforated web. However, the buckling stress was found to follow a solid plate‚Äôs stress value, and
will therefore not be considered in the following discussion.
6.1
ModiÔ¨Ācations of EC3 for Compression Members
All the calculations for the eÔ¨Äective widths follow Eurocode 3 Part 1-1 and Part 1-3, with references
to Eurocode 3 Part 1-5. The gross-sectional properties take into account the perforations in the web,
and, additionally, a check of the eÔ¨Äective heights of the web is made, to insure that they are smaller
than the solid parts of the slotted web (so and su):
h1eff =min (h1eff, so)
(6.1a)
h2eff =min (h2eff, su)
(6.1b)
Another assumption is that strips in the slotted part of the web subjected to compression undergo
local buckling, so they are not part of the eÔ¨Äective cross-section. While strips that are in tension are
assumed to be fully eÔ¨Äective.
The rotational spring stiÔ¨Äness of the slotted web that was derived in Section 3 is incorporated in
Eurocode 3 to estimate the failure load of a slotted member in compression. Cőł (Equation 3.4 and
3.9) is to enter the calculations through Equation 2.3a.
As mentioned in Section 2.3.1, the reduced thickness of the web is used to estimate the rotational
spring stiÔ¨Äness. The reduced thickness corresponds to the reduction in web height for local buckling,
but applied to the thickness for this calculation.
The Ô¨Ānal modiÔ¨Ācation is to the Euler buckling load, which is used to estimate the reduction factor, Ōá,
for the relevant buckling mode. This factor is then used in the Ô¨Ānal strength prediction, see Equation
2.7. An expansion of the Euler buckling load was made in Section 4.5, where the shear Ô¨āexibility was
included in the buckling load. This modiÔ¨Ācation is used in the Eurocode calculations, on its own and
together with the rotational spring stiÔ¨Äness of the web.
6.2
Comparison to compression test
Both Shaker (2015b) and Jacobsen (2015b) performed compression tests of slotted cold-formed steel
members. The test set ups are slightly diÔ¨Äerent: how the stud members are supported. The failure
loads will be compared to the predicted strengths using modiÔ¨Āed Eurocode calculations, and due to
excellent work by Jacobsen, 2015b and Shaker, 2015b, the actual dimensions, and to some extent the
actual material properties of the members, will be used for the estimation of the Ô¨Ānal strength. All
the dimensions, tested failure loads, predicted strengths, and some material properties are listed in
Appendix B.
52
==PAGE:65==
Modeling of Slotted Cold-Formed Steel Members
Employing ModiÔ¨Ācations into Eurocode 3
DiÔ¨Äerent versions of Eurocode 3 will be used in the calculations presented below. The same notation
for the Eurocode version will be used in this section:
‚ÄĘ EC3 - Eurocode 3 without modiÔ¨Ācations, but which considers the slots in the web for gross-
sectional properties and for checking the eÔ¨Äective heights
‚ÄĘ EC3 ‚ąí1 - Eurocode 3 with the rotational spring stiÔ¨Äness of the web
‚ÄĘ EC3 ‚ąí2 - Eurocode 3 with shear Ô¨āexibility term in the Euler buckling load
‚ÄĘ EC3 ‚ąí3 - Eurocode 3 with both rotational spring stiÔ¨Äness and shear Ô¨āexibility
6.2.1
Pure Compression Tests by Shaker
The experiments performed by Shaker (2015b) are almost identical with the experiments mentioned in
Section 2.3.3. 18 tests on slotted CFS members with diÔ¨Äerent lengths and thicknesses were executed.
Figure 6.1: End support for the experiments performed by Shaker (2015b). A groove is cut into the
support for better grip of the tested member. Picture from Shaker (2015b).
The experiments were on lipped channel members subjected to pure compression and supported by a
custom-built support, which served as a simple end condition, see Figure 6.1. It is assumed that the
axial force is applied evenly on the member, meaning that second order eÔ¨Äects will not be included.
All the specimens were measured, and the actual dimensions are used in the calculations. Five dog
bone tests were executed on each member thickness, however, only the material properties for the
thick members (t = 2mm) were used for the calculations. The material properties are listed in Table
6.1 together with the nominal dimensions of the members. The actual dimensions of the members are
listed in Appendix B.2. The length, thickness and number of slots are described for each test series
in Table 6.2.

and three diÔ¨Äerent modiÔ¨Ācations of Eurocode 3 are listed in Table 6.2. Note that Eurocode 3 without

6.2.2
Compression Tests by Jacobsen
Twelve compression tests were performed by Jacobsen (2015b) on slotted lipped channel members, all
have the same nominal dimensions and lengths. Six of the experiments were supported at mid height
against horizontal displacements, see Figure 6.2. The stud members were attached by screws to a
track member at bottom and top, which was connected to the compression machine, see Figure 6.3.
All the specimens were measured, and the actual dimensions were used in the calculations. Likewise,
dog bone test were executed on each member, so the actual material properties are available for all the
members, though, one measurement is missing, but is not used in the calculations (fu). The average
material properties are listed in Table 6.3 together with the nominal dimensions of the members,
while the measured dimensions are available in Appendix B.3. The thickness, the half wavelength,
and number of slots are described for each test series in Table 6.4.
Figure 6.2:
Illustration of the support at mid
height. From Jacobsen (2015b).
Figure 6.3: Illustration of the end support of the
column. From Jacobsen (2015b).
From the Ô¨Ārst set of Eurocode 3 calculations, where second order eÔ¨Äects are included, it was found
that the predicted strengths were slightly greater than the failure loads. Therefore, another set of
calculations was made, where it was assumed that the boundary conditions of the column transfer the
loads to the web and not to the elastic center of the member. This change of load application point
results in greater second order eÔ¨Äects for the minor bending axis and lower predicted strengths.

6.2.3
Fabrication Imperfections
The experiments performed by Shaker, 2015b on both solid and slotted CFS members can give insight
to the imperfections due to manufacturing, as in variation of dimensions. Variations of the dimensions
in the cross-section do have an impact on the performance of the member, which was investigated in
Fischer and Schafer (2016).
The manufacturing process of slotted cold-formed steel members has an additional step, which can
provide additional oÔ¨Ä-nominal dimensions. It can be assumed that the cutting, or punching machine
has a Ô¨Āxed cutting pattern, so no variation in the perforations occours. But the placement of the sheet
for punching or cutting can be slightly oÔ¨Ä, resulting in an angled line of the perforations. The cutting
or punching of the slots can also result in minor damages of the strips between the slots.
All added up, it is not surprising that the slotted members have slightly larger variations in the tested
capacity, see Table 6.6.
Table 6.6: Average and standard deviation of the tested capacities of solid and slotted CFS members in com-
pression, from Shaker (2015b).
Solid CFS
Slotted CFS

6.3
Transverse Loaded Slotted CFS Members
Frederiksen and Spange (1992) and Borglund and Jonsson (1997) performed experimental tests on
slotted CFS members in compression, bending and a combination of bending and compression. The
test specimens are slightly diÔ¨Äerent from the member illustrated in Figure 2.3, leading to a new
formulation for the rotation of the perforated web. Additionally, the specimens are screwed to plaster
boards, on one or both sides. The plaster boards have an impact on the Ô¨Ānal capacity of the system,
according to Telue and Mahendran (1999) and Schafer and Hiriyur (2002b).
Below the changes of the Eurocode 3 calculations will be described brieÔ¨āy for considering the new
types of slotted lipped channel members. The strength of the tested members will be predicted using
two versions of the Eurocode 3 calculations:
‚ÄĘ EC3 - Eurocode 3 which considers the perforations in the web for determining the cross-sectional
properties, and checking of the eÔ¨Äective heights of the web, to insure they are smaller than the
solid part of the web
‚ÄĘ EC3m - Eurocode 3, as described above and including the perforated web‚Äôs rotational stiÔ¨Äness
The predicted strengths of the members will be presented and compared to the test results.
‚ąóOne test result is excluded from this average and standard deviation, the test result is much lower than the average
value. According to the test report this member was subjected to a higher load rate, and the test was ended before
failure.

(6.5)
These capacities are used in the combined bending and compression condition, found in Equation 2.9,
for getting the axial compression capacity. Iteration of the applied normal force, elastic center and
reduction factors for distortional buckling is done until convergence is reached. The results of this
study are also listed in Table 6.9 together with the capacities of the steel sections without sheathings.
The calculations can be found in Appendix D.4.
Six diÔ¨Äerent test series, with diÔ¨Äerent test setups, were executed, however, only results from four test
series are listed in Table 6.9. All the members were placed horizontally, so even the pure compression
tests were subjected to moments from the self weight. A brief description of each test series is provided
below and an illustration of the test setups is shown in Figure 6.6.
‚ÄĘ A - Compression until failure. The members are subjected to self weight, two tests were per-
formed of test specimens with thickness 1.0mm.
‚ÄĘ B - Eccentric compression until failure. The load is applied in the midpoint of the top Ô¨āange,
resulting in a moment with eccentricity equal to the distance from top Ô¨āange to the eÔ¨Äective
elastic center, ez. Six tests were performed, two of each thickness and self weight adds to the
applied moment.
‚ÄĘ E - Concentric compression until failure with a distributed load at 1.27 kN
m and self weight. Six
tests were performed, two specimens of each thickness.
‚ÄĘ F - Transverse loading till failure. Two specimens with thickness 1mm.
Figure 6.6: Illustration of the test series performed by Borglund and Jonsson (1997). Picture from
H√∂glund (1997).
The results in Table 6.9 are divided into four groups, as how to explain the failures of the wall
systems. The Ô¨Ārst group comprises the thin CFS members (0.7mm). The predicted strengths of the
steel sections without contribution of the sheathings are closer to the tested failure loads, therefore it
is assumed that it is the CFS members that cause the failure. The sheathing will of course provide
some contribution to the capacity, which will add to the EC3 predictions and thereby get closer to the
actual failure load. The observed failures of these members are buckling of the compression Ô¨āange.
The next group comprises the members with thickness 1mm and the thick members in test series B.
In this group it is assumed that the whole system fails and not just the steel members, therefore, the
predicted strength including the strength of the sheathings will be the best estimate for the failure
loads. Note that the failure load to predicted strength ratio is around one for this group, which veriÔ¨Āes
the assumption.

7.4
Plate Buckling Compression Stress
Investigation of the plate buckling stress of a slotted web was executed in Section 5, and it revealed
that the critical buckling stress for a solid plate is the best representation for the slotted web as
well. The alternative buckling stress was derived for a homogeneous orthotropic material, which had
a bending stiÔ¨Äness corresponding to the bending stiÔ¨Äness of the slotted plate in the longitudinal and
transverse direction.

Modeling of Slotted Cold-Formed Steel Members
Conclusion
8
Conclusion
The perforated cold-formed steel member has been the subject of investigation of its stiÔ¨Äness and
derivation of appropriate formulas for use in connection to design procedures for slotted cold-formed
steel members. The investigated members have the shape of a lipped channel member, with perfora-
tions in the web section.
The lipped channel member is often aÔ¨Äected by distortional buckling, which is handled in Eurocode 3
with a reduction factor. This factor is estimated from the spring stiÔ¨Äness of the edge stiÔ¨Äener, in which
the stiÔ¨Äness of the web is part. However, the perforations reduce the stiÔ¨Äness of the web, therefore
an expression for the slotted web‚Äôs rotational stiÔ¨Äness is derived and, by parametric study of the
dimensions in the perforated web, veriÔ¨Āed. Two diÔ¨Äerent stiÔ¨Änesses are proposed for use in connection
to the distortional buckling reduction factor in Eurocode 3: One for a compression member and one
for a member subjected to major axis bending.
The bearing capacity of slotted cold-formed steel members has been estimated using Eurocode 3 and
the proposed model for the web‚Äôs stiÔ¨Äness. The capacities are compared to test results of compressed
slotted cold-formed steel members conducted prior to this report. It is found that using this method
in general yields slightly conservative results, compared to the test‚Äôs capacities.
The web of the lipped channel member has been exposed prior to investigations. A reduced shear
stiÔ¨Äness of the slotted web was derived, based on deformations and stress distributions of FEM models
subjected to shear deformations. The shear stiÔ¨Äness model is compared to several FEM models of
diÔ¨Äerent layouts of the slotted web. The proposed model yields the correct magnitude of stiÔ¨Äness,
but does not capture the proper changes in stiÔ¨Äness for varying dimensions. The calculated stiÔ¨Änesses
deviate up to 600% from the FEM results. However, the deviations are much smaller for realistic
dimensions of the perforations, where the results diÔ¨Äer up to 300%.
A Ô¨Ānal investigation of the perforated web‚Äôs stability issues was executed. The perforated web is
assumed to behave as a homogeneous orthotropic material, where the bending stiÔ¨Äness in the lon-
gitudinal and transverse direction considers the reduced stiÔ¨Äness due to the slots, and the twisting
stiÔ¨Äness uses the reduced shear stiÔ¨Äness that was derived earlier. The plate buckling stress for an
orthotropic plate was derived and compared to FEM results of a slotted web subjected to longitudinal
compression. However, it was found that critical stresses from a homogeneous isotropic material Ô¨Ātted
the stresses from the FEM models much better than the orthotropic results.
A similar study was performed for the critical shear stresses of an isotropic and orthotropic plate.
Surprisingly, this study showed that the stresses from the plates with orthotropic properties predicted
the critical shear stresses much better than the isotropic material.
The stability study of the compression stress provides evidence that critical stress can with ease be used
to estimate the eÔ¨Äective widths for pure compression members on slotted cold-formed steel members.
However, for veriÔ¨Ācation of shear stability issues, the critical shear stress should be estimated for a
plate with orthotropic properties that consider the perforations in the web.
Perforated cold-formed steel members have been subjected to a combination of compression, bending
and transverse loading. The test specimens were built-up wall sections with attached gypsum boards.
An estimation of the capacity of the wall sections was compared to the test results and to some extent
found to be comparable.
71
==PAGE:84==
Modeling of Slotted Cold-Formed Steel Members
Conclusion
8.1
Perspectivation
This project has investigated the stiÔ¨Äness of the slotted web in diÔ¨Äerent scenarios. Many of the models
capture the strength of the slotted web, but some are found to be rough estimates, like the model for
the perforated web‚Äôs shear stiÔ¨Äness, which should be subjected to further studies resulting in a new
or modiÔ¨Āed model.
The best way for verifying calculation models is to compare with test results, therefore more experi-
mental tests on various perforated members (without sheathings) subjected to compression, bending,
transverse loading, or combinations thereof are desirable. They could provide the appropriate veriÔ¨Ā-
cation of the proposed models: For the web‚Äôs rotational spring stiÔ¨Äness for a member in major axis
bending and collapses due to shear stability of the perforated web.
J√łrgen Sloth Nielsen from Knauf A/S revealed that research to Ô¨Ānd an optimized layout of the perfo-
rations is currently in process. The optimization is to Ô¨Ānd the best match between thermal properties
and the strength of the member. The variables are the layout of the perforations: Sizes, locations,
direction of the slots, etc.
Finally, the direct strength method was initially developed to escape the eÔ¨Äective width calculations,
why it is possible that the method can be modiÔ¨Āed to include predictions of perforated members.
Maybe, the critical buckling loads can be estimated from orthotropic plate properties, and used in the
current strength prediction formula.

Diagonal matrix:
bmn:=(m,n)-> a*b*Pi^4/(32)*(Dx*(m/a)^4+Dy*(n/b)^4+2*(D1+2*Dxy)*
(m/a)^2*(n/b)^2): 'M[dia]'=bmn(m,n)

a2 b2
Off-diagonal matrix:
bpq:=(m,n,p,q)-> `if`((type(n+q, odd)), `if`((type(m+p, odd)), m*
n*p*q/((m^2-p^2)*(q^2-n^2)), 0), 0 ): 
ni:=2: mi:=2:
mn := Matrix(ni*mi,1): count:=0:
for i from 1 to mi do
  for j from 1 to ni do
    count:=count+1: 
    mn[count,1] := i,j:
  end do
end do
Mo := Matrix(ni*mi,ni*mi):
for i from 1 to mi*ni do
  for j from 1 to ni*mi do
    Mo[i,j] := bpq(mn(i),mn(j)):
  end do
end do
Md := Matrix(ni*mi,ni*mi):
for i from 1 to mi*ni do
    Md[i,i] := bmn(mn(i)):
end do
Full matrix:
simplify(Md/N+Mo)

Listing F.6: Python Script for Plate Buckling, Shear - Abaqus Models

==PAGE:199==
Modeling of Slotted Cold-Formed Steel Members
Appendix G.1
G
Matlab scripts
Two Matlab scripts was made of the Eurocode 3 calculations for the solid and slotted lipped channel
member with the layour in Figure 2.3. The scripts iterate over the eÔ¨Äective cross-sectional properties,
distortional buckling factors till convergence is reached.
The stresses in the cross-section is determined from the eÔ¨Äective properties.
The slotted script includes the modiÔ¨Ācations of EC3 as Method(1)=1 is using the slotted web‚Äôs rota-
tional stiÔ¨Äness, Method(3)=1 is including the shear term to the Euler buckling load.
Both scripts can handle any combination of loading, compression, major/minor bending. But only
solves the critical capacity of one load type. The calculations corresponds to the Mathcad documents
of EC3 of solid and slotted members (Appendix D.1 and D.2).
G.1

Preface
Preface
This project was written as a literature review preparing for the following Master‚Äôs thesis at the
Department of Civil Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark. The work was done under
supervision of Professor Michael J. Andreassen at DTU.
The work was performed in the fall 2015 at DTU with a project weighting of 5 ECTS points. The
project is part of the author‚Äôs Master degree at the Technical University of Denmark.
Astrid Winther Fischer
Kgs. Lyngby, December 2015

Modeling of Slotted Cold-Formed Steel Members

211
==PAGE:224==
Slotted Cold-Formed Steel Members
Literature Review
Litterature Review
Cold formed steel (CFS) members are construction elements, which have been used in the past few
decades in the US and Australia, while in Scandinavia the slotted CFS member is more frequently
used, due to its better thermal properties. Since the introduction of CFS members to the industry,
researchers have been determined to Ô¨Ānd improved methods for predicting the strength of steel mem-
bers with thinner cross-sections. Over time, several methods have been used and even more presented,
especially, have researchers been focused on methods for determining the capacities of solid CFS mem-
bers. This has lead to that cold-formed steel is a ‚Äônew‚Äô branch of steel work with its own set of theories
and terminology.
CFS members with a slotted web is less studied, due to its later introduction and lesser widespread
use, which might also explain why no speciÔ¨Ācation in Eurocode is available for predicting the capacity
of slotted CFS members. Getting a consisting model for strength prediction of slotted CFS members
is of great interest, and attempts have been made to solve this problem, but a strong close-form model
is yet to come.
A variety of diÔ¨Äerent papers and reports about CFS have been reviewed for clariÔ¨Ācation of old and new
methods used for predicting the strength of CFS members. Several methods have been widely used
and modiÔ¨Āed by other authors, and some methods are consistently recommended for use in future.
Special interest from the author is directed towards the distortional buckling and towards the slotted
CFS member, which will get special attention in the author‚Äôs Ô¨Ānal Master‚Äôs Thesis, where the Ô¨Ānal
goal is to set up models for determining the capacities of slotted CFS-members, in axial compression,
major/minor bending and for shear loading.
Each reviewed paper and report are summarized in the second part of this review, where the major
problems, solutions and results are mentioned. In the Ô¨Ārst part, below, the actual summary of the
papers and reports are presented according to what the literature‚Äôs introduced, used or investigated
method is determining. So papers, which refer to methods to predict the local buckling load will be
presented together.
Local Buckling
Schafer (1998 and 1999b) propose a ‚Äôsemi-empirical interaction‚Äô model for determining the local buck-
ling stress of a CFS member in compression or Ô¨āexure. The method consider the interaction between
sub-elements, an alternative method is the ‚Äôelement model‚Äô, which determines the strength of each
sub-element of the member. The proposed model perform much better than the current ‚ÄôElement
model‚Äô, giving a predicted strength closer to FSM-results and smaller variations. [33, 36] (Article
no.# 3 and 5)
A complex summation of current, proposed and another suggested strength prediction methods for
local, distortional and global buckling are presented by Schafer (2002).
Experimental tests were
performed by other researchers, and the performance of the methods are listed according to the used
strength prediction method. The methods presented are anywhere between the DSM, ‚Äôelement model‚Äô,
Hancock‚Äôs method for distortional buckling, semi-empirical interaction model, and rotation stiÔ¨Äness
for the distortional buckling. Final recommendation is found to be the prediction methods C2 and
C3, which use DSM with the ‚Äôsemi-empirical interaction‚Äô model for estimating the local buckling load
or FSM results, respectively. It is found that these methods perform best, and no eÔ¨Äective width

calculations are needed. [28] (Article no.# 26)
Schafer (1996) proposed an analytical method for determining the capacity of CFS members with web
stiÔ¨Äeners. [32] (Article no.# 19) Rasmussen (2006) made a model for determining the global buckling
stress for a member which is already in a local buckling state. Two loading cases, pure compression
and combination of compression and bending, are described in the paper, but more cases are referred
to. [23] (Article no.# 16)
Distortional Buckling
A proposed model for the distortional buckling prediction is presented by Schafer (1998 and 1999b),
where the rotational stiÔ¨Äness of the Ô¨āange and web is determined and used in:
fed = kŌÜfe + kŌÜwe
kŌÜfg + kŌÜwg
The method yields conservative strengths for members in bending, but not for members in pure
compression, when the values are compared to FSM results. The results are close to the FSM results
and have a small variation. [33, 36] (Article no.# 3 and 5)
Lau and Hancock (1994 and 1997) introduced and veriÔ¨Āed a method for predicting the distortional
buckling capacity of members in bending or compression. The results are compared to experimental
data of members undergoing distortional buckling, and it was found that the method predict the
strength quite well, however, for one set of data the predicted strengths are a bit to much on the
unconservative side. DiÔ¨Äerent methods for predicting the distortional buckling load for members in
pure bending, are compared to the FSM models, and it is found that the method proposed by Hancock
gives the best Ô¨Āt, with slightly conservative predicted results. [8, 7] (Article no.# 41 and 43
Analytical expressions for the distortional buckling load is derived in Lau and Hancock (1987). Final,
simpliÔ¨Āed expressions are compared to FSM results, and gives good conformity. [19] (Article no.# 42
The bachelor thesis by Ebbesen and Jacobsen (2014) made compression tests of CFS members. The
predicted failure stresses are based on Eurocode 3: part 1-3 for ‚Äôlip stiÔ¨Äener‚Äô distortional buckling
(Ô¨āange-web rotation stiÔ¨Äness), which overall match the failure stresses of the tested specimens, some
deviations are present for some of the tested members. [6] (Article no.# 11)
The paper by Schafer (2002), lists methods for determining the Ô¨Ānal predicted strength of CFS mem-
bers, where the Ô¨Ānal recommendation is the prediction methods C2, and C3, which are the DSM with
hand calculations or FSM results for determining the buckling loads. It is found that these methods
perform best compared to test results. The hand calculations for the buckling loads is the proposed
method by Schafer (1999b) where the rotational stiÔ¨Äness of the Ô¨āange and web is determined for
estimating the buckling stress. [28] (Article no.# 26)
Kesti (1999c and 2000) compared results from the methods listed in EC3, ‚ÄôHancock‚Äôs method (used
in AS/NZS) and the method proposed by Schafer and Pek√∂z (1998 and 1999b) with results from
GBT. It was found that the ‚ÄôHancock‚Äôs method gives on average 4% lower results than GBT, while
the EC3 method gives more conservative results, Schafer‚Äôs method give 3% lower results. ‚ÄôHancock‚Äôs
method give a much smaller variation in predicted strengths compared to EC3.
A modiÔ¨Āed EC3
method is suggested, were buckling loads from GBT is used for determining the eÔ¨Äective sectional
properties, followed by determining the strength of the member. The modiÔ¨Āed EC3 and original EC3
2

Literature Review
are compared to test results, and the modiÔ¨Āed EC3 method is found to give a good correlation with
the test results, with a smaller variation between the predicted-test ratios. The diÔ¨Äerence between the
methods decreases with increasing member lengths, where the boundary conditions have less inÔ¨āuence
on the deformation and the global buckling is more dominating. [17, 18, 15] (Article no.# 9, 13 and
14)
Kesti (2000) studied the inÔ¨āuence of diÔ¨Äerent boundary conditions on the distortional buckling load,
and the eÔ¨Äect web stiÔ¨Äeners do have on the distortional buckling mode. [15] (Article no.# 9)
The direct strength method (DSM) was Ô¨Ārst proposed by Schafer (1998) which was an alternative
method to the AISI approach. Three diÔ¨Äerent setups of the distortional buckling strength was pre-
sented, and compared to experiments performed by other researchers. One method perform overall
as good as the AISI method, but with a smaller variation. In Schafer (2006) the up-dated DSM is
presented and describes the method for using DSM for design. [37, 29] (Article no.# 21 and 25)
Slotted CFS Members
H√∂glund (1997, 1998a and 1998b) and Th√∂yr√§ (2001) presents current strength prediction methods for
slotted CFS members. The analytical predicted strengths are compared with test results, which yield
good comparison for members in pure compression, less good comparison for members in combined
bending and compression, where the predicted strength is on the conservative side. For members
in pure bending the predicted strengths are very conservative. Another set of tests, the predicted
strengths and failure modes do correspond to the tested results, but still with conservative predictions.
[10, 9, 11, 42] (Article no.# 6, 8 and 10)
Additionally, Th√∂yr√§ (2001) compare test results (performed by Kesti (1999)) with FEM results and
results from TNN ‚Äôa Computer program for thin-walled sections‚Äô. The results from the test and TNN
are found to give conservative results, because the program does not take the slotted part into account.
[42] (Article no.# 6)
Kesti (2000) studied the inÔ¨āuence the slots in the webs have on the local and distortional buckling
loads, where reduced thickness factors for local and distortional buckling are listed and are comparable
to reduction factors for bending stiÔ¨Äness of the web part. Experimental results were compared to EC3,
Hancock‚Äôs and Schafers method, where good comparison between tests and Hancock‚Äôs method was
evident. Schafer‚Äôs method did perform well as well, while the EC3 method is largely conservative. For
web stiÔ¨Äened slotted elements, the EC3 method did perform remarkably better than both Schafer‚Äôs
and Hancock‚Äôs method. [15] (Article no.# 9)
Borglund and Jonsson (1997) performed compression tests with or without transverse loading of CFS
members with and without sheathing.
The test results were compared to the prediction method
introduced by H√∂glund (1997, 1998a and 1998b).
It was found that the prediction model is an
accurately enough tool for predicting the strength of slotted members, especially, when some of the
input parameters can reÔ¨āect reality more accurately. [3] (Article no.# 32)
Salamoun (2015) conducted 18 pure compression tests on slotted CFS members. The results were
compared to the design method listed in EC3 for solid CFS members. EC3 predicted strength both
larger and smaller than the test results. Shaker (2015) and Jacobsen(2015) performed experiments
on slotted CFS members and compared the results to both FEM models and a new method for use
in EC3 method for determining the distortional buckling load. It was found that the modiÔ¨Āed EC3
method gives reasonable results, though with a large scatter and some unconservative predictions.

==PAGE:227==
Slotted Cold-Formed Steel Members
Literature Review
[13, 38, 24] (Article no.# 34, 35 and 37)
Shear Failure
Th√∂yr√§ (2001) presents shear buckling and shear strength predictions formulas for slotted CFS mem-
bers. Comparison between the predicted strengths with results obtained from two sets of tested slotted
CFS members in shear, give good conformity, however on the conservative side, with a small variance.
[42] (Article no.# 6)
Connections
Costa (1999) (and Th√∂yr√§ (2001)) tested 7 diÔ¨Äerent support types for sheathed slotted CFS members,
in total 18 tests. The test results are compared to results obtained by analytical hand calculations,
which are not presented clearly, and to SVE ‚Äôa computer program for analysis of beams of two parts
and an elastic shear medium‚Äô. Comparing the hand calculations with the test results yield a good
conformity for most of the tested supports, expect for one set of tests. [42, 5] (Article no.# 6 and 7)
Lyng and Persson (2014) tested 40 slotted CFS studs with connection to a track member. Comparing
the test results with calculations according to EN 1993-1-3:2007 give some reasonable conformity,
where EC3 is conservative, when simple supports are considered. Finally, a FEM model was made
and buckling mode was similar to the buckling mode for the tested member. A detailed connection
FEM model was made by Madsen (2015) and compared to the test results performed by Lyng and
Persson (2014). The buckling mode of the FEM model was not obtained but the load transferring
could be compared and studied. [2, 21, 22] (Article no.# 1, 2 and 40)
Imperfections
De Sena Cardoso (2015) made a large investigation of diÔ¨Äerent variations in CFS systems: Material
properties (yield stress, Young modulus), fabrication tolerances, geometric imperfections, joint stiÔ¨Äness
and loads.
Not all of the uncertainties where included in his Ô¨Ānal analysis of portal frames and
storage rack frames, but the imperfections of the out of plumb angle of the frame is considered as
a random normal variable, likewise was the member imperfections also modeled (local, distortional,
bow, chamber and twist imperfections). [4] (Article no.# 22)
Zhao (2015) has the last few years set up a laser scanner for a full 3D model of specimens and its
imperfections. It is possible to get the full member exported to Abaqus, or analyse imperfections from
the scanned specimens. [43] (Article no.# 27)
Schafer (1996 and 1998) measured imperfections of CFS members, and categorized the imperfections
into two groups, local and distortional imperfections. Suggesting that the maximum imperfection
can be treated as a random variable, or by use of a modal imperfection spectrum.
Additionally,
investigation of the residual stresses was made, and recommend residual stresses to be used in an
analysis. [35, 34] (Article no.# 4 and 18)

==PAGE:228==

Literature Review
Miscellaneous
Schafer (2006) and Li (2010) present the CUFSM program and updated features, like general boundary
conditions, and cFSM. Proposals how to use CUFSM and cFSM with DSM. [20, 30] (Article no.# 23
and 24)
Schafer (2002) and Telue and Mahendran (1999) studied the inÔ¨āuence of sheathed stud members‚Äô
bearing performance, and found that members with sheathing have a remarkable increase in stability
and strength. The Ô¨Ānal recommendation is that the standard should include the increase in strength
for studs with sheaths, Telue and Mahendran propose eÔ¨Äective lengths to account for the increase
in strength due to the sheathing. Slotted sheathed studs were tested by Kesti (2000) and compared
to analytical predictions where additional rotation stiÔ¨Äness is added in the calculations, which gave
really good conformity. [37, 41, 15] (Article no.# 9, 21 and 29)
Sarawit and Pek√∂z (2000) compare the performance of two diÔ¨Äerent approaches for predicting the
strength of members with diÔ¨Äerent conÔ¨Āguration of stiÔ¨Äening system of CFS members. It was found
that a method proposed by Schafer and Pek√∂z (1999) gave reasonable result for members with diÔ¨Äerent
types of stiÔ¨Äeners then simple lips. [26] (Article no.# 28)
H√∂glund (1998) presented the slotted CFS member, with all its properties and features: increased
insulation and sound proofness, vibration performance and Ô¨Āre protection. [9] (Article no.# 8)
Th√∂yr√§ (2001) did Ô¨Āre testing on sheathed slotted CFS members in compression and compared to
three diÔ¨Äerent analytical strength prediction methods, for diÔ¨Äerent buckle modes. [42] (Article no.#
6)
Final Remarks
The most recommended method is for predicting the distortional buckling load through Hancock‚Äôs
method. FSM, FEM, GBT and experiments have all lead to a recommendation of this method above
the EC3 method and Schafer‚Äôs method. For local buckling the semi-empirical method by Schafer, have
also found its way through many authors recommendations, as a stronger prediction method than the
EC3‚Äôs element model. Three diÔ¨Äerent methods for slotted CFS member have been mentioned in the
reviewed papers, the one presented by H√∂glund, which predicts conservative strengths for the slotted
members. The reduced thickness by Kesti and the modiÔ¨Āed EC3 method for distortional buckling.

Schafer, B.W. and T. Pek√∂z (1999b) Local and Distortional Buck-
ling of Cold-Formed Steel Members with Edge-StiÔ¨Äened Flanges
Local buckling, distortional buckling, CFS design, imperfection sensitivity
Proposing two new methods for predicting the local and distortional buckling loads, and comparing
with the current methods at that time - ‚Äôelement method‚Äô and the eÔ¨Äective width method. The old
and new methods are tested and compared to a Ô¨Ānite strip model.
Local buckling: The current method ignores the interactions between elements by just considering
an isolated plate with pinned support resulting in that each element/plate is predicted to buckle at
diÔ¨Äerent stresses. New suggested method is the ‚Äôsemi-empirical interaction model‚Äô, which accounts for
the interaction between two elements. This is found ‚Äôthat the minimum local buckling stress of any
two attached elements is a reasonable approximation of the entire member local buckling stress‚Äô. It was
found by comparison that the ‚Äôelement method‚Äô resulted in conservative predictions, while the use of
the ‚Äôsemi-empirical interaction model‚Äô is generally a reasonable local buckling predictor.
Distortional buckling: The current methods do not explicitly threat the distortional mode and the
methods for capacity predictions are cumbersome, due to the issue related to the rotational restraint at
the juncture of Ô¨āange and web. The distortional buckling is more sensitive to the rotational restraint
than the local buckling. The new method is performed in a manner similar to a method by Hancock.
By comparison to a Ô¨Ānite strip model, the new method was found to be a reasonable predictor, but
with errors. But for cases with slender webs the new method correctly converge to the web‚Äôs local
buckling stress, while Hancock‚Äôs method converge to zero stress.
The post-buckling capacity is smaller for the distortional mode than for the local buckling capacity.
Distortional failures are also more sensitive to initial imperfections than local failures.
The current speciÔ¨Ācation involves calculating the eÔ¨Äective area for calculating the local buckling alone
and determine reduced section properties. The new method suggest to consider possible interactions
of distortional buckling in global modes, and then calculate an eÔ¨Äective width, which accounts for
local and distortional buckling. Thus, another method is suggested for predicting the failure mode
and reducing the post-buckling capacity in the distortional mode. [33]

variations in the maximum imperfections exists, and one way to treat the imperfections are as a
random variable.

on CFS members along the length with a direct current diÔ¨Äerential transformer (DCDT) on a drill
