Adequate computational modeling of residual stresses is troublesome for analysis and can be com-

Schafer, B.W. and T. Pek√∂z (1998a) Laterally Braced Cold-Formed
Steel Flexural Members with Edge StiÔ¨Äened Flanges
CFS design, distortional buckling
Current design methods for CFS members do take local buckling into account, but not distortional
buckling. Experiments and numerically analysis show that distortional buckling is required for pre-
dicting a members strength. AISI attempts to account for distortional buckling through an empirical
reduction of the local plate buckling coeÔ¨Écient. Experiments demonstrate unconservative predictions
of ASIS speciÔ¨Ācation.
A hand method for calculating the distortional buckling stress was proposed by Hancook, and an
extended method was made for predicting the strength in distortional buckling.
Comparing this
method with test data, shows that it is better, but still overly conservative. An uniÔ¨Āed treatment
of the local and distortional buckling is wanted. Stating with closed form predicting the local and
distortional buckling stress, where interactions between web, lip and Ô¨āanges are considered.
Distortional buckling mode does have a smaller post-buckling capacity compared to local buckling.
And distortional buckling do have a greater inÔ¨āuence on the failure mode, even though it has a larger
critical stress than the local mode.
Analytical models are needed for determining the rotational stiÔ¨Äness from Ô¨āanges and web.
For
predicting the critical buckling stress in distortional mode a method is found to be the best by hand
calculations. 32 members are examined by FSM for comparing with the proposed methods. For local
buckling the interaction model performs better than the element model, which is too conservative.
The post-buckling behavior of local and distortional modes are analyzed in ABAQUS, which shows

that the Ô¨Ānal failure mode do resembles the distortional mode, even in cases with a lower local buckling

ignore interactions between elements in the cross-section. It is also diÔ¨Écult to predict the distortional
buckling mode for complicated cross-sections.
Semi-analytical Ô¨Ānite strip methods are used in this work. 574 experiments are collected and compared
to results of the elastic buckling found by FSM. Test data and determined results are compared, and
there is a clear trend. Three new prediction methods and the AISI speciÔ¨Ācation are compared to
the test data. The AISI speciÔ¨Ācation do have large variation of results, and do not have a design
for members with web stiÔ¨Äeners. The Ô¨Ārst suggested method, is conservative, the variation is smaller
than the AISI. The second method works poorly, and the third method performs well. The method is
accurate and more reliable than the other direct strength methods and is even better than method in
AISI. [37]
22
Cardoso, F.M.H.de Sena (2015) System Reliability-Based Cri-
teria for Designing Cold-Formed Steel Structures by Advanced
Analysis
CFS design, systems, reliability, Monte Carlo
Numerical models and full scale tests were performed on two diÔ¨Äerent CFS structures. Monte Carlo
stimulation was used to get some statistical results, when variances on the material properties, im-
perfections etc were considered as well. The results are then used to make some design criteria for
CFS systems, which will save time and the designer will be more aware of the systems performance as
a whole. It was also found that designing as a hole system do save material, compared to designing
member for member.
All matters of imperfections, or variance on CFS members or structures are listed, but only a small
portion of the variances are taken into account. Most are assumed to be equal to the design values.
One of them is the tolerances on the CFS members, but they are not considered, only the variance on
the thickness. [4]

Li, Z. and B.W. Schafer (2010) Buckling Analysis of Cold-Formed
Steel Members with General Boundary Conditions using CUFSM:

CFS design, CUFSM, cFSM, DSM
New update of CUFSM, so it can handle general boundary conditions of CFS members. The paper
compare a bit results for a clamped-clamped member in major bending. Else the paper details the
theory and the implementation of the new functions in CUFSM.
Constrained FSM (cFSM) is also described, how it works and how to solve the problems using cFSM.

Schafer, B.W. and S. √Ād√°ny (2006) Buckling Analysis of Cold-

strained Finite Strip Methods
CUFSM, cFSM
Walk through of the theory behind FSM/CUFSM. Setting up the form functions, stiÔ¨Äness matrices
and solving the buckling problem. The CUFSM program at this stage can only handle simple supports
of the member, due to much more complicated solutions of the problem if general supports should be
included.
Additionally, the constrained method are also described: how it works, and the implementation of this
method into FSM. The author tells about the strong and weak sides of the results obtained by cFSM.

Schafer, B.W. (2006) Designing Cold-Formed Steel using the
Direct Strength Method
CUFSM, DSM, CFS design
Introduction and presenting the DSM AISI Design Guide for use of professionals for designing CFS
structures. DSM is easier to use than the EÔ¨Äective Width method, which is still a part of the AISI
SpeciÔ¨Ācation, and DSM is a new additional method in the speciÔ¨Ācation. DSM do have some limitations:
It can only determine the axial and bending strengths of a member, it does not consider shear, web
crippling, members with holes, and it do not consider increased strength for the work of cold forming.
DSM is very conservative for slender members. The rest is some results from DSM and how it works,
and how to use CUFSM for getting the buckling loads. [29]

26
Schafer, B.W. (2002) Local, Distortional and Euler Buckling of
Thin-Walled Columns
CFS design, summarizing methods, comparing methods and tests
The paper summarizes methods for predicting the capacity of CFS members: for local buckling closed
form solutions are listed with the element approach and the semi-empirical approach. Close form
solutions for distortional buckling are derived by Schafer and Pekoz, where the rotation stiÔ¨Äness is
estimated before actual capacity prediction.
The accuracy of the closed form predictions are compared with FSM analysis. where the local buck-
ling semi-empirical model is more accurate than the element model. Current distortional buckling
predictions are Ô¨āawed and a method by Hancock and Schafer work better.
The ultimate strength, as mentioned in many other articles, depends on the local and distortional
buckling: Distortional buckling failure have lower post buckling capacity than local buckling failure
and distortional buckling is more sensitive to imperfections.
Finally, the methods are compared to tests, and the performance of the tests are discussed. It is
found that method C2 and C3 perform well, which is the direct strength method. ‚ÄôThe method uses
separate column curves for local buckling [Eq. (19)] and distortional buckling [Eq. (16)], with the
slenderness and maximum capacity in each mode controlled by consideration of Euler buckling [Eq.
(18)]. The method considers all the buckling modes in a consistent manner, does not require eÔ¨Äective
width calculations, and demonstrates that numerical elastic buckling solutions (e.g., Ô¨Ānite strip) may
be used as the key input to determining the strength of a large variety of thin-walled compression
members.‚Äô [28]

Measurement of Cold-Formed Steel Members:
Model Recon-
struction and Application
Imperfections
Description of the laser scanner for setting up a 3D model of a member with all imperfections. How the

information out of the model: bow imperfections, measurements of the dimensions, and transforming
the model to a ABAQUS Ô¨Āle. [43]

Jacobsen, J.H. (2015a) Slotted Thin-Walled Steel Members -
Analytical, Numerical and Experimental Study of Wall Columns
Slotted CFS, tests, comparing to EC3, design method distortional buckling
Twelve pure compression column tests were performed, six with a midpoint lateral support and six with
no midpoint support. Comparison of test results with Eurocode design method, where the reduction
in stiÔ¨Äness due to the slotted part of the web is considered, by a new method to calculate the rotation
stiÔ¨Äness between Ô¨āange and web. The method is proposed by the author. A FEM model is also made
for comparison.
The method is based on calculating the rotation at the Ô¨āange/web junction, when the web is considered
as a simple supported plate. Then dividing the rotation by the unit moment. From this the rotation
stiÔ¨Äness can be estimated.
It was found that all the tested columns failed in an interactional global-distortional buckling mode.
The midpoint lateral support provided an increase of capacity to the double strength. For the long
column with no intermediate support, all the results are similar, where the FEM model gives an
conservative estimate, while the Eurocode gives an unconservative capacity. For the column with
midpoint support, the results diÔ¨Äer much, and cannot be compared, where the Eurocode gives the

Madsen, P.V. (2015) Slotted Cold-Formed Steel Members - A
Numerical Analysis of the Behavior of the Joints in an Exterior
Slotted Load Bearing Wall System
Connection details, FEM model
Together with J.H, Jacobsen (2015a) and M.N. Shaker (2015a) an alternative method for determining
the rotational stiÔ¨Äness was introduced, in this thesis the analytical method was compared to FEM
models. The results from M.B. Lyng and K. Persson (2014) experiments were reused in this thesis
and compared to a detailed FEM model. The experiments are compression tests of slotted lipped
channel columns, connected to track members, for capture the traditional connection details in a CFS
building.
The FEM model is made with contact surfaces between diÔ¨Äerent components, and includes the screws
in the test setup between the stud and track members.
It was found that the FEM model gives
reasonable results, but do not give the full collapse load nor mode.
Nevertheless, the behavior of the connection detail can be analysed from the FEM model, and it is
found that the screws transfer most of the loads initial, until yield, where the load transferred is
constant, while the contact surface between stud and track the load transferred increases during the
compression ‚Äôtest‚Äô. [22]

Hancock, G.J., K.B. Young and E.S. Bernard (1994) Strength

Buckling
CFS design, distortional buckling, comparing with tests and FSM
‚ÄôTwo sets of design curves for cold-formed sections undergoing distortional buckling have been proposed
and compared with the test results. The Ô¨Ārst set is based on an eÔ¨Äective section approach whilst the
second provides a prediction of the maximum stress in the distortional buckling mode including the
postbuckling reserve of slender sections. Both approaches were found to produce a reasonable estimate
of the results for sections which underwent distortional buckling before or at the same time as local
buckling. For sections which undergo local buckling before distortional buckling, a separate method
accounting for local buckling should be performed.‚Äô [8]
